	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a list of British prime ministers, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">List of Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Head of UK Government</div>

<p>The <b>Prime Minister of the United Kingdom</b> (informally abbreviated to <b>PM</b>), until 1801 known as the <b>Prime Minister of Great Britain</b>, is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head</a> of <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">government of the United Kingdom</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Minister">Prime Minister</a> directs both the executive and the legislature, and, together with the Prime Minister's <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a>, (consisting of all the most senior <a href="/wiki/Minister_(government)" title="Minister (government)">ministers</a>, most of whom are government department heads), is <a href="/wiki/Accountability" title="Accountability">accountable</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">Monarch</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a>, to the Prime Minister's <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of political parties in the United Kingdom">political party</a> and, ultimately, to the <a href="/wiki/Election#Suffrage" title="Election">electorate</a> for the policies and actions of the executive and the legislature.
</p><p>The office is not established by any statute or constitutional document but exists only by long-established <a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)#Government" title="Convention (norm)">convention</a>, whereby the monarch appoints as Prime Minister the person most likely to command the <a href="/wiki/Motions_of_no_confidence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Motions of no confidence in the United Kingdom">confidence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> this individual is typically the leader of the political party or <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition</a> of parties that holds the largest number of seats in that chamber. The position of Prime Minister was not created; it evolved slowly and erratically over three hundred years due to numerous acts of Parliament, political developments, and accidents of history. The office is therefore best understood from a historical perspective. The origins of the position are found in constitutional changes that occurred during the Revolutionary Settlement (1688–1720) and the resulting shift of political power from the Sovereign to Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Evolution_of_Prime_Minister_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evolution_of_Prime_Minister-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Although the Sovereign was not stripped of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal prerogative in the United Kingdom">ancient prerogative powers</a> and legally remained the head of government, politically it gradually became necessary for him or her to govern through a Prime Minister who could command a majority in Parliament.
</p><p>By the 1830s the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a> of government (or cabinet government) had emerged; the Prime Minister had become <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i> or the first among equals in the Cabinet and the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> in the United Kingdom. The political position of Prime Minister was enhanced by the development of modern political parties, the introduction of mass communication and photography. By the start of the 20th century the modern premiership had emerged; the office had become the pre-eminent position in the constitutional hierarchy vis-à-vis the Sovereign, Parliament and Cabinet.
</p><p>Prior to 1902, the Prime Minister sometimes came from the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, provided that his government could form a majority in the Commons. However as the power of the aristocracy waned during the 19th century the convention developed that the Prime Minister should always sit in the lower house. As leader of the House of Commons, the Prime Minister's authority was further enhanced by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a> which marginalised the influence of the House of Lords in the law-making process.
</p><p>The Prime Minister is <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_officio" class="mw-redirect" title="Ex officio">ex officio</a></i> also <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_the_Civil_Service" title="Minister for the Civil Service">Minister for the Civil Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_the_Union" title="Minister for the Union">Minister for the Union</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Certain privileges, such as <a href="/wiki/List_of_residents_of_10_Downing_Street" title="List of residents of 10 Downing Street">residency</a> of <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a>, are accorded to Prime Ministers by virtue of their position as First Lord of the Treasury. The status of the position as Prime Minister means that the incumbent is consistently ranked as one of the most powerful and influential people in the world.
</p>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authority">Authority</span></h2>
<p>The Prime Minister is the head of <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">the United Kingdom government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As such, the modern Prime Minister leads the Cabinet (the Executive). In addition, the Prime Minister leads a major political party and generally commands a majority in the House of Commons (the lower House of the legislature). The incumbent wields both significant legislative and executive powers. Under the British system, there is a unity of powers rather than <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the House of Commons, the Prime Minister guides the law-making process with the goal of enacting the legislative agenda of their political party. In an executive capacity, the Prime Minister appoints (and may dismiss) all other Cabinet members and <a href="/wiki/Minister_(government)" title="Minister (government)">ministers</a>, and co-ordinates the policies and activities of all government departments, and the staff of the Civil Service. The Prime Minister also acts as the public "face" and "voice" of Her Majesty's Government, both at home and abroad. Solely upon the advice of the Prime Minister, the Sovereign exercises many statutory and prerogative powers, including high judicial, political, official and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> ecclesiastical appointments; the conferral of peerages and some knighthoods, decorations and other important honours.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of the United Kingdom.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" decoding="async" width="32" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/48px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/64px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Kingdom" title="Portal:United Kingdom">United Kingdom&#32;portal</a>  <div class="hlist" style="text-align:center;font-weight:normal;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_political_science#Politics_by_region" title="Outline of political science">Other countries</a></li>
<li><a href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Atlas_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="extiw" title="commons:Atlas of the United Kingdom">Atlas</a></li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Template talk:Politics of the United Kingdom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Politics_of_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Constitution of the United Kingdom">Constitution of the United Kingdom</a></div><p>
The British system of government is based on an <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Constitution of the United Kingdom">uncodified constitution</a>, meaning that it is not set out in any single document.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The British constitution consists of many documents and most importantly for the evolution of the Office of the Prime Minister, it is based on customs known as <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_convention_(political_custom)" title="Constitutional convention (political custom)">constitutional conventions</a> that became accepted practice. In 1928, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> described this characteristic of the British constitution in his memoirs:</p><blockquote><p>In this country we live ... under an unwritten Constitution. It is true that we have on the Statute-book great instruments like Magna Carta, the Petition of Right, and the Bill of Rights which define and secure many of our rights and privileges; but the great bulk of our constitutional liberties and ... our constitutional practices do not derive their validity and sanction from any Bill which has received the formal assent of the King, Lords and Commons. They rest on usage, custom, convention, often of slow growth in their early stages, not always uniform, but which in the course of time received universal observance and respect.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The relationships between the Prime Minister and the Sovereign, Parliament and Cabinet are defined largely by these unwritten conventions of the constitution. Many of the Prime Minister's executive and legislative powers are actually <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogatives</a> which are still formally vested in the <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Monarchy of the United Kingdom">Sovereign</a>, who remains the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Despite its growing dominance in the constitutional hierarchy, the premiership was given little formal recognition until the 20th century; the <a href="/wiki/Legal_fiction" title="Legal fiction">legal fiction</a> was maintained that the Sovereign still governed directly. The position was first mentioned in statute only in 1917, in the schedule of the <a href="/wiki/Chequers_Estate_Act_1917" title="Chequers Estate Act 1917">Chequers Estate Act</a>. Increasingly during the 20th century, the office and role of Prime Minister featured in <a href="/wiki/Statute_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Statute law">statute law</a> and official documents; however, the Prime Minister's powers and relationships with other institutions still largely continue to derive from ancient royal prerogatives and historic and modern constitutional conventions. Prime ministers continue to hold the position of <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a> and, since November 1968, that of <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_the_Civil_Service" title="Minister for the Civil Service">Minister for the Civil Service</a>, the latter giving them authority over the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Civil_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Civil Service">civil service</a>.
</p><p>Under this arrangement, Britain might appear to have two executives: the Prime Minister and the Sovereign. The concept of "<a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a>" resolves this paradox.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The Crown symbolises the state's authority to govern: to make laws and execute them, impose taxes and collect them, declare war and make peace. Before the "<a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>" of 1688, the Sovereign exclusively wielded the powers of the Crown; afterwards, Parliament gradually forced monarchs to assume a neutral political position. Parliament has effectively dispersed the powers of the Crown, entrusting its authority to responsible ministers (the Prime Minister and Cabinet), accountable for their policies and actions to Parliament, in particular the elected <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a>.
</p><p>Although many of the Sovereign's prerogative powers are still legally intact,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> constitutional conventions have removed the monarch from day-to-day governance, with ministers exercising the royal prerogatives, leaving the monarch in practice with three constitutional rights: to be kept informed, to advise, and to warn.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundations">Foundations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_Settlement">Revolutionary Settlement</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a></div>
<p>Because the Premiership was not intentionally created, there is no exact date when its evolution began. A meaningful starting point, however, is 1688–89 when <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James II</a> fled <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">Parliament of England</a> confirmed William and Mary as joint constitutional monarchs, enacting legislation that limited their authority and that of their successors: the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights (1689)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mutiny_Acts" title="Mutiny Acts">Mutiny Bill</a> (1689), the <a href="/wiki/Triennial_Acts" title="Triennial Acts">Triennial Bill</a> (1694), the <a href="/wiki/Treason_Act_1695" title="Treason Act 1695">Treason Act (1696)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Settlement_1701" title="Act of Settlement 1701">Act of Settlement (1701)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Known collectively as the Revolutionary Settlement, these acts transformed the constitution, shifting the balance of power from the Sovereign to Parliament. They also provided the basis for the evolution of the office of Prime Minister, which did not exist at that time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treasury_Bench">Treasury Bench</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg/220px-British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg/330px-British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg/440px-British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_House_of_Commons_1834.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Late in the 17th century Treasury Ministers began to attend the Commons regularly. They were given a reserved place, called the Treasury Bench, to the Speaker's right where the Prime Minister and senior Cabinet members sit today.</div></div></div>
<p>The Revolutionary Settlement gave the Commons control over finances and legislation and changed the relationship between the Executive and the Legislature. For want of money, Sovereigns had to summon Parliament annually and could no longer dissolve or prorogue it without its advice and consent. Parliament became a permanent feature of political life.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The veto fell into disuse because Sovereigns feared that if they denied legislation, Parliament would deny them money. No Sovereign has denied <a href="/wiki/Royal_assent" title="Royal assent">royal assent</a> since Queen <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Anne</a> vetoed the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Militia_Bill" title="Scottish Militia Bill">Scottish Militia Bill</a> in 1708.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Treasury officials and other department heads were drawn into Parliament serving as liaisons between it and the Sovereign. Ministers had to present the government's policies, and negotiate with Members to gain the support of the majority; they had to explain the government's financial needs, suggest ways of meeting them and give an account of how money had been spent. The Sovereign's representatives attended Commons sessions so regularly that they were given reserved seats at the front, known as the Treasury Bench. This is the beginning of "unity of powers": the Sovereign's Ministers (the Executive) became leading members of Parliament (the Legislature). Today the Prime Minister (<a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> (responsible for <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_budget" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom budget">The Budget</a>) and other senior members of the Cabinet sit on the Treasury bench and present policies in much the same way Ministers did late in the 17th century.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standing_Order_66">Standing Order 66</span></h3>
<p>After the Revolution, there was a constant threat that non-government members of Parliament would ruin the country's finances by proposing ill-considered money bills. Vying for control to avoid chaos, the Crown's Ministers gained an advantage in 1706, when the Commons informally declared, "That this House will receive no petition for any sum of money relating to public Service, but what is recommended from the Crown." On 11 June 1713, this non-binding rule became Standing Order 66: that "the Commons would not vote money for any purpose, except on a motion of a Minister of the Crown." Standing Order 66 remains in effect today (though renumbered as no. 48),<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> essentially unchanged for three hundred years.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Empowering Ministers with sole financial initiative had an immediate and lasting impact. Apart from achieving its intended purpose – to stabilise the budgetary process – it gave the Crown a leadership role in the Commons; and, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Treasurer" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Treasurer">Lord Treasurer</a> assumed a leading position among Ministers.
</p><p>The power of financial initiative was not, however, absolute. Only Ministers might initiate money bills, but Parliament now reviewed and consented to them. Standing Order 66 therefore represents the beginnings of Ministerial responsibility and accountability.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Prime Minister" appears at this time as an unofficial title for the leader of the government, usually the Head of the Treasury.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Jonathan Swift, for example, wrote in 1713 about "those who are now commonly called Prime Minister among us", referring to <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Godolphin,_1st_Earl_of_Godolphin" title="Sidney Godolphin, 1st Earl of Godolphin">Sidney Godolphin, 1st Earl of Godolphin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Harley,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Earl_Mortimer" title="Robert Harley, 1st Earl of Oxford and Earl Mortimer">Robert Harley</a>, Queen Anne's Lord Treasurers and chief ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Since 1721, every head of the Sovereign's government – with one exception in the 18th century (<a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">William Pitt the Elder</a>) and one in the 19th (<a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a>) – has been First Lord of the Treasury.
</p>
<h3><span id="Beginnings_of_the_Prime_Minister.27s_party_leadership"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_the_Prime_Minister's_party_leadership">Beginnings of the Prime Minister's party leadership</span></h3>
<p>Political parties first appeared during the <a href="/wiki/Exclusion_Crisis" title="Exclusion Crisis">Exclusion Crisis</a> of 1678–1681. The <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whigs</a>, who believed in <a href="/wiki/Limited_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Limited monarchy">limited monarchy</a>, wanted to exclude <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, Duke of York</a>, from succeeding to the throne because he was a Roman Catholic. The <a href="/wiki/Tories_(British_political_party)" title="Tories (British political party)">Tories</a>, who believed in the "<a href="/wiki/Divine_Right_of_Kings" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine Right of Kings">Divine Right of Kings</a>", defended James's hereditary claim.
</p><p>Political parties were not well organised or disciplined in the 17th century. They were more like factions, with "members" drifting in and out, collaborating temporarily on issues when it was to their advantage, then disbanding when it was not. A major deterrent to the development of opposing parties was the idea that there could only be one "King's Party" and to oppose it would be disloyal or even treasonous. This idea lingered throughout the 18th century. Nevertheless  it became possible at the end of the 17th century to identify Parliaments and Ministries as being either "Whig" or "Tory" in composition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet">Cabinet</span></h3>
<p>The modern Prime Minister is also the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet</a>. A convention of the constitution, the modern Cabinet is a group of ministers who formulate policies.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett249_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett249-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> As the political heads of government departments Cabinet Ministers ensure that policies are carried out by permanent civil servants. Although the modern Prime Minister selects Ministers, appointment still rests with the Sovereign.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> With the Prime Minister as its leader, the Cabinet forms the <a href="/wiki/Executive_branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive branch">executive branch</a> of government.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "Cabinet" first appears after the Revolutionary Settlement to describe those ministers who conferred privately with the Sovereign. The growth of the Cabinet met with widespread complaint and opposition because its meetings were often held in secret and it excluded the ancient <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Honourable_Privy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Most Honourable Privy Council">Privy Council</a> (of which the Cabinet is formally a committee) from the Sovereign's circle of advisers, reducing it to an honorary body.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The early Cabinet, like that of today, included the Treasurer and other department heads who sat on the Treasury bench. However, it might also include individuals who were not members of Parliament such as household officers (e.g. the Master of the Horse) and members of the royal family. The exclusion of non-members of Parliament from the Cabinet was essential to the development of ministerial accountability and responsibility.
</p><p>Both William and Anne appointed and dismissed Cabinet members, attended meetings, made decisions, and followed up on actions. Relieving the Sovereign of these responsibilities and gaining control over the Cabinet's composition was an essential part of evolution of the Premiership. This process began after the Hanoverian Succession. Although <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">George I</a> (1714–1727) attended Cabinet meetings at first, after 1717 he withdrew because he did not speak fluent English and was bored with the discussions. <a href="/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain" title="George II of Great Britain">George II</a> (1727–1760) occasionally presided at Cabinet meetings but his grandson, <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">George III</a> (1760–1820), is known to have attended only two during his 60-year reign. Thus, the convention that Sovereigns do not attend Cabinet meetings was established primarily through royal indifference to the everyday tasks of governance. The Prime Minister became responsible for calling meetings, presiding, taking notes, and reporting to the Sovereign. These simple executive tasks naturally gave the Prime Minister ascendancy over his Cabinet colleagues.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the first three Hanoverians rarely attended Cabinet meetings they insisted on their prerogatives to appoint and dismiss ministers and to direct policy even if from outside the Cabinet. It was not until late in the 18th century that Prime Ministers gained control over Cabinet composition (see section <a href="#Emergence_of_Cabinet_government">Emergence of Cabinet Government</a> below).
</p>
<h3><span id=".22One_Party_Government.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;One_Party_Government&quot;">"One Party Government"</span></h3>
<p>British governments (or Ministries) are generally formed by one party. The Prime Minister and Cabinet are usually all members of the same political party, almost always the one that has a majority of seats in the House of Commons. Coalition governments (a ministry that consists of representatives from two or more parties) and minority governments (a one-party ministry formed by a party that does not command a majority in the Commons) were relatively rare before the 2010 election, since 2010 there has been both a coalition and minority government. "One party government", as this system is sometimes called, has been the general rule for almost three hundred years.
</p><p>Early in his reign, <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William III</a> (1689–1702) preferred "Mixed Ministries" (or coalitions) consisting of both Tories and Whigs. William thought this composition would dilute the power of any one party and also give him the benefit of differing points of view. However, this approach did not work well because the members could not agree on a leader or on policies, and often worked at odds with each other.
</p><p>In 1697, William formed a homogeneous Whig ministry. Known as the <i>Junto</i>, this government is often cited as the first true Cabinet because its members were all Whigs, reflecting the majority composition of the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Anne (1702–1714) followed this pattern but preferred Tory Cabinets. This approach worked well as long as Parliament was also predominantly Tory. However, in 1708, when the Whigs obtained a majority, Anne did not call on them to form a government, refusing to accept the idea that politicians could force themselves on her merely because their party had a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> She never parted with an entire Ministry or accepted an entirely new one regardless of the results of an election. Anne preferred to retain a minority government rather than be dictated to by Parliament. Consequently, her chief ministers <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Godolphin,_1st_Earl_of_Godolphin" title="Sidney Godolphin, 1st Earl of Godolphin">Sidney Godolphin, 1st Earl of Godolphin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Harley,_1st_Earl_of_Oxford_and_Earl_Mortimer" title="Robert Harley, 1st Earl of Oxford and Earl Mortimer">Robert Harley</a>, who were called "Prime Minister" by some, had difficulty executing policy in the face of a hostile Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith,_pages_379–382_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith,_pages_379–382-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>William's and Anne's experiments with the political composition of the Cabinet illustrated the strengths of one party government and the weaknesses of coalition and minority governments. Nevertheless, it was not until the 1830s that the constitutional convention was established that the Sovereign must select the Prime Minister (and Cabinet) from the party whose views reflect those of the majority in Parliament. Since then, most ministries have reflected this one party rule.
</p><p>Despite the "one party" convention, prime ministers may still be called upon to lead either minority or coalition governments. A <a href="/wiki/Minority_government" title="Minority government">minority government</a> may be formed as a result of a "<a href="/wiki/Hung_parliament" title="Hung parliament">hung parliament</a>" in which no single party commands a majority in the House of Commons after a general election or the death, resignation or defection of existing members. By convention the serving Prime Minister is given the first opportunity to reach agreements that will allow them to survive a vote of confidence in the House and continue to govern. Until 2017, the last minority government was led by Labour Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> for eight months after the <a href="/wiki/February_1974_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="February 1974 United Kingdom general election">February 1974 general election</a> produced a hung parliament. In the <a href="/wiki/October_1974_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="October 1974 United Kingdom general election">October 1974 general election</a>, the Labour Party gained 18 seats, giving Wilson a majority of three.
</p><p>A hung parliament may also lead to the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition government</a> in which two or more parties negotiate a joint programme to command a majority in the Commons. Coalitions have also been formed during times of national crisis such as war. Under such circumstances, the parties agree to temporarily set aside their political differences and to unite to face the national crisis. Coalitions are rare: since 1721, there have been fewer than a dozen.
</p><p>When the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2010 United Kingdom general election">general election of 2010</a> produced a hung parliament, the Conservative and Liberal Democrat parties agreed to form the <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron–Clegg coalition">Cameron–Clegg coalition</a>, the first coalition in seventy years. The previous coalition in the UK before 2010 was led by Conservative Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> during most of the Second World War, from May 1940 to May 1945. <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, the leader of the Labour Party, served as deputy Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/2015_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="2015 United Kingdom general election">general election of 2015</a>, the nation returned to one party government after the Tories won an outright majority.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treasury_Commission">Treasury Commission</span></h3>
<p>The Premiership is still largely a convention of the constitution; its legal authority is derived primarily from the fact that the Prime Minister is also First Lord of the Treasury. The connection of these two offices – one a convention, the other a legal office – began with the Hanoverian Succession in 1714.
</p><p>When George I succeeded to the British throne in 1714, his German ministers advised him to leave the office of <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Treasurer" title="Lord High Treasurer">Lord High Treasurer</a> vacant because those who had held it in recent years had grown overly powerful, in effect, replacing the Sovereign as head of the government. They also feared that a Lord High Treasurer would undermine their own influence with the new King. They therefore suggested that he place the office in "commission", meaning that a committee of five ministers would perform its functions together. Theoretically, this dilution of authority would prevent any one of them from presuming to be the head of the government. The King agreed and created the Treasury Commission consisting of the First Lord of the Treasury, the Second Lord, and three Junior Lords.
</p><p>No one has been appointed Lord High Treasurer since 1714; it has remained in commission for three hundred years. The Treasury Commission ceased to meet late in the 18th century but has survived, albeit with very different functions: the First Lord of the Treasury is now the Prime Minister, the Second Lord is the Chancellor of the Exchequer (and actually in charge of the Treasury), and the Junior Lords are government <a href="/wiki/Whip_(politics)" title="Whip (politics)">Whips</a> maintaining party discipline in the House of Commons; they no longer have any duties related to the Treasury, though when <a href="/wiki/Delegated_legislation_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Delegated legislation in the United Kingdom">subordinate legislation</a> requires the consent of the Treasury it is still two of the Junior Lords who sign on its behalf.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_prime_ministers">Early prime ministers</span></h2>
<h3><span id=".22First.22_prime_minister"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;First&quot;_prime_minister">"First" prime minister</span></h3>
<p>Since the office evolved rather than being instantly created, it may not be totally clear-cut who the first prime minister was. However, this appellation is traditionally given to Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a>, who became <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> in 1721.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg/170px-Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg/255px-Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg/340px-Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg 2x" data-file-width="920" data-file-height="1476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo_-_Robert_Walpole.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a>, studio of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_van_Loo" title="Jean-Baptiste van Loo">Jean-Baptiste van Loo</a>, 1740. Walpole is generally considered to have been Britain's first Prime Minister.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1720, the <a href="/wiki/South_Sea_Company" title="South Sea Company">South Sea Company</a>, created to trade in cotton, agricultural goods and slaves, collapsed, causing the financial ruin of thousands of investors and heavy losses for many others, including members of the royal family. King George I called on Robert Walpole, well known for his political and financial acumen, to handle the emergency. With considerable skill and some luck, Walpole acted quickly to restore public credit and confidence, and led the country out of the crisis. A year later, the king appointed him First Lord of the Treasury, Chancellor of the Exchequer, and Leader of the House of Commons – making him the most powerful minister in the government. Ruthless, crude, and hard-working, he had a "sagacious business sense" and was a superb manager of men.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> At the head of affairs for the next two decades, Walpole stabilised the nation's finances, kept it at peace, made it prosperous, and secured the <a href="/wiki/Hanoverian_Succession" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanoverian Succession">Hanoverian Succession</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Walpole demonstrated for the first time how a chief minister – a prime minister – could be the actual head of the government under the new constitutional framework. First, recognising that the sovereign could no longer govern directly but was still the nominal head of the government, he insisted that he was nothing more than the "King's Servant".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Second, recognising that power had shifted to the Commons, he conducted the nation's business there and made it dominant over the Lords in all matters. Third, recognising that the Cabinet had become the executive and must be united, he dominated the other members and demanded their complete support for his policies. Fourth, recognising that political parties were the source of ministerial strength, he led the Whig party and maintained discipline. In the Commons, he insisted on the support of all Whig members, especially those who held office. Finally, he set an example for future Prime Ministers by resigning his offices in 1742 after a <a href="/wiki/Vote_of_confidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote of confidence">vote of confidence</a>, which he won by just three votes. The slimness of this majority undermined his power, even though he still retained the confidence of the Sovereign.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ambivalence_and_denial">Ambivalence and denial</span></h3>
<p>For all his contributions, Walpole was not a prime minister in the modern sense. The king – not Parliament – chose him; and the king – not Walpole – chose the Cabinet. Walpole set an example, not a precedent, and few followed his example. For over 40 years after Walpole's fall in 1742, there was widespread ambivalence about the position. In some cases, the prime minister was a figurehead with power being wielded by other individuals; in others there was a reversion to the "chief minister" model of earlier times in which the sovereign actually governed.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> At other times, there appeared to be two prime ministers. During <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain_in_the_Seven_Years%27_War" title="Great Britain in the Seven Years&#39; War">Britain's participation in the Seven Years' War</a>, for example, the powers of government were divided equally between the <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pelham-Holles,_1st_Duke_of_Newcastle" title="Thomas Pelham-Holles, 1st Duke of Newcastle">Duke of Newcastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt,_1st_Earl_of_Chatham" title="William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham">William Pitt, 1st Earl of Chatham</a>, leading to them both alternatively being described as prime minister. Furthermore, many thought that the title "Prime Minister" usurped the sovereign's constitutional position as "head of the government" and that it was an affront to other ministers because they were all appointed by and equally responsible to the sovereign.
</p><p>For these reasons, there was a reluctance to use the title. Although Walpole is now called the "first" prime minister, the title was not commonly used during his tenure. Walpole himself denied it. In 1741, during the attack that led to Walpole's downfall, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sandys,_1st_Baron_Sandys" title="Samuel Sandys, 1st Baron Sandys">Samuel Sandys</a> declared that "According to our Constitution we can have no sole and prime minister". In his defence, Walpole said "I unequivocally deny that I am sole or Prime Minister and that to my influence and direction all the affairs of government must be attributed".<sup id="cite_ref-Marriott,_p._88_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marriott,_p._88-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Grenville" title="George Grenville">George Grenville</a>, Prime Minister in the 1760s, said it was "an odious title" and never used it.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frederick_North,_Lord_North" title="Frederick North, Lord North">Lord North</a>, the reluctant head of the King's Government during the <a href="/wiki/American_War_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="American War of Independence">American War of Independence</a>, "would never suffer himself to be called Prime Minister, because it was an office unknown to the Constitution".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Denials of the premiership's legal existence continued throughout the 19th century. In 1806, for example, one member of the Commons said, "the Constitution abhors the idea of a prime minister". In 1829, <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Lansdowne" title="Marquess of Lansdowne">Lord Lansdowne</a> said, "nothing could be more mischievous or unconstitutional than to recognise by act of parliament the existence of such an office".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the turn of the 20th century the premiership had become, by convention, the most important position in the constitutional hierarchy. Yet there were no legal documents describing its powers or acknowledging its existence. The first official recognition given to the office had only been in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a> in 1878, when <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Disraeli</a> signed as "First Lord of the Treasury and Prime Minister of her Britannic Majesty".<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett,_p._245_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett,_p._245-50">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> It wasn't until seven years later, in 1885, did the official records entrench the institution of prime minister, using "Prime Minister" at the list of government ministers printed in <a href="/wiki/Hansard" title="Hansard">Hansard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Incumbents had no statutory authority in their own right. As late as 1904, Arthur Balfour explained the status of his office in a speech at Haddington: "The Prime Minister has no salary as Prime Minister. He has no statutory duties as Prime Minister, his name occurs in no Acts of Parliament, and though holding the most important place in the constitutional hierarchy, he has no place which is recognised by the laws of his country. This is a strange paradox."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1905 the position was given some official recognition when the "Prime Minister" was named in the <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_precedence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders of precedence in the United Kingdom">order of precedence</a>, outranked, among non-royals, only by the Archbishops of <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">York</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moderator_of_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="Moderator of the General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">Moderator of the General Assembly of the Church of Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Act of Parliament to mention the premiership – albeit in a schedule – was the <a href="/wiki/Chequers_Estate_Act_1917" title="Chequers Estate Act 1917">Chequers Estate Act</a> on 20 December 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This law conferred the <a href="/wiki/Chequers" title="Chequers">Chequers Estate</a> owned by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Lee,_1st_Viscount_Lee_of_Fareham" title="Arthur Lee, 1st Viscount Lee of Fareham">Sir Arthur</a> and Lady Lee, as a gift to the Crown for use as a country home for future prime ministers.
</p><p>Unequivocal legal recognition was given in the <a href="/wiki/Ministers_of_the_Crown_Act_1937" title="Ministers of the Crown Act 1937">Ministers of the Crown Act 1937</a>, which made provision for payment of a salary to the person who is both "the First Lord of the Treasury and Prime Minister". Explicitly recognising two hundred years' of ambivalence, the Act states that it intended "To give statutory recognition to the existence of the position of prime minister, and to the historic link between the premiership and the office of First Lord of the Treasury, by providing in respect to that position and office a salary of ..." The Act made a distinction between the "position" (prime minister) and the "office" (First Lord of the Treasury), emphasising the unique political character of the former. Nevertheless, the brass plate on the door of the prime minister's home, <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a>, still bears the title of "First Lord of the Treasury", as it has since the 18th century as it is officially the home of the First Lord and not the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>P 34</span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Union_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland.2C_1801"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland,_1801">Union of Great Britain and Ireland, 1801</span></h2>
<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1798" title="Irish Rebellion of 1798">Irish Rebellion of 1798</a>, the British prime minister, <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>,  believed the solution to rising Irish nationalism was a union of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a>. Britain then included <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, but Ireland had its own parliament and government, which were firmly <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish">Anglo-Irish</a> and did not represent the aspirations of most Irishmen. For this and other reasons, Pitt advanced his policy, and after some difficulty in persuading the Irish political class to surrender its control of Ireland under the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_1782" title="Constitution of 1782">Constitution of 1782</a>, the new union was created by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Acts of Union 1800</a>. With effect from 1 January 1801, Great Britain and Ireland were united into a single kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Ireland" title="Parliament of Ireland">Parliament of Ireland</a> came to an end, and until 1922 British ministers were responsible for all three kingdoms of the British Isles.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_Treaty" title="Anglo-Irish Treaty">Anglo-Irish Treaty</a> of 6 December 1921, which was to be put into effect within one year, the enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State_Constitution_Act_1922" title="Irish Free State Constitution Act 1922">Irish Free State Constitution Act 1922</a> was concluded on 5 December 1922, creating the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a>, who had been in office as prime minister of Great Britain and Ireland for only six weeks, and who had just won the <a href="/wiki/1922_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1922 United Kingdom general election">general election of November 1922</a>, thus became the last prime minister whose responsibilities covered both Britain and the whole of Ireland. Most of a parliamentary session beginning on 20 November was devoted to the Act, and Bonar Law pushed through the creation of the Free State in the face of opposition from the "die hards".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id=".22First_among_equals.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;First_among_equals&quot;">"First among equals"</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_Cabinet_government">Emergence of Cabinet government</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cabinet of the United Kingdom">Cabinet of the United Kingdom</a></div>
<p>Despite the reluctance to legally recognise the Premiership, ambivalence toward it waned in the 1780s. During the first 20 years of his reign, George III (1760–1820) tried to be his own "prime minister" by controlling policy from outside the Cabinet, appointing and dismissing ministers, meeting privately with individual ministers, and giving them instructions. These practices caused confusion and dissension in Cabinet meetings; King George's experiment in personal rule was generally a failure. After the failure of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_North,_Lord_North" title="Frederick North, Lord North">Lord North</a>'s ministry (1770–1782) in March 1782 due to Britain's defeat in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Vote_of_no_confidence" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote of no confidence">vote of no confidence</a> by Parliament, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Watson-Wentworth,_2nd_Marquess_of_Rockingham" title="Charles Watson-Wentworth, 2nd Marquess of Rockingham">Marquess of Rockingham</a> reasserted the Prime Minister's control over the Cabinet. Rockingham assumed the Premiership "on the distinct understanding that measures were to be changed as well as men; and that the measures for which the new ministry required the royal consent were the measures which they, while in opposition, had advocated." He and his Cabinet were united in their policies and would stand or fall together; they also refused to accept anyone in the Cabinet who did not agree.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup> King George threatened to <a href="/wiki/Abdication" title="Abdication">abdicate</a> but in the end reluctantly agreed out of necessity: he had to have a government.
</p><p>From this time, there was a growing acceptance of the position of Prime Minister and the title was more commonly used, if only unofficially.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith,_pages_379–382_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith,_pages_379–382-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Associated initially with the Whigs, the Tories started to accept it. Lord North, for example, who had said the office was "unknown to the constitution", reversed himself in 1783 when he said, "In this country some one man or some body of men like a Cabinet should govern the whole and direct every measure."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In 1803, <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>, also a Tory, suggested to a friend that "this person generally called the first minister" was an absolute necessity for a government to function, and expressed his belief that this person should be the minister in charge of the finances.<sup id="cite_ref-Marriott,_p._88_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marriott,_p._88-45">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg/220px-The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg/330px-The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg/440px-The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1740" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_House_of_Commons_1793-94_by_Karl_Anton_Hickel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>William Pitt the Younger addressing the House of Commons.</i> Pitt's 19 years as Prime Minister followed by Lord Liverpool's 15, led the Tory Party to accept the office as a convention of the constitution.</div></div></div>
<p>The Tories' wholesale conversion started when Pitt was confirmed as Prime Minister in the election of 1784. For the next 17 years until 1801 (and again from 1804 to 1806), Pitt, the Tory, was Prime Minister in the same sense that Walpole, the Whig, had been earlier.
</p><p>Their conversion was reinforced after 1810. In that year, George III, who had suffered periodically from mental instability (possibly due to a blood disorder now known as <a href="/wiki/Porphyria" title="Porphyria">porphyria</a>), became permanently insane and spent the remaining 10 years of his life unable to discharge his duties. The <a href="/wiki/Prince_Regent" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Regent">Prince Regent</a> was prevented from using the full powers of Kingship. The Regent became <a href="/wiki/George_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George IV of the United Kingdom">George IV</a> in 1820, but during his 10-year reign was indolent and frivolous. Consequently, for 20 years the throne was virtually vacant and Tory Cabinets led by Tory Prime Ministers filled the void, governing virtually on their own.
</p><p>The Tories were in power for almost 50 years, except for a Whig ministry from 1806 to 1807. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Jenkinson,_2nd_Earl_of_Liverpool" title="Robert Jenkinson, 2nd Earl of Liverpool">Lord Liverpool</a> was Prime Minister for 15 years; he and Pitt held the position for 34 years. Under their long, consistent leadership, Cabinet government became a convention of the constitution. Although subtle issues remained to be settled, the <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">Cabinet system of government</a> is essentially the same today as it was in 1830.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><div class="noresize"><map name="ImageMap_c44d445fd273a19c"><area href="/wiki/Robert_Lowe,_1st_Viscount_Sherbrooke" shape="circle" coords="30,99,15" alt="Robert Lowe &#x2013; Chancellor" title="Robert Lowe &#x2013; Chancellor" /><area href="/wiki/John_Bright" shape="circle" coords="65,96,14" alt="John Bright &#x2013; Board of Trade" title="John Bright &#x2013; Board of Trade" /><area href="/wiki/George_Campbell,_8th_Duke_of_Argyll" shape="circle" coords="96,95,10" alt="George Campbell, Duke of Argyll &#x2013; India" title="George Campbell, Duke of Argyll &#x2013; India" /><area href="/wiki/George_Villiers,_4th_Earl_of_Clarendon" shape="circle" coords="131,89,11" alt="George Villiers, Earl of Clarendon &#x2013; Foreign Affairs" title="George Villiers, Earl of Clarendon &#x2013; Foreign Affairs" /><area href="/wiki/Henry_Bruce,_1st_Baron_Aberdare" shape="circle" coords="150,87,10" alt="Henry Bruce, Baron Aberdare &#x2013; Home Secretary" title="Henry Bruce, Baron Aberdare &#x2013; Home Secretary" /><area href="/wiki/William_Wood,_1st_Baron_Hatherley" shape="circle" coords="172,90,8" alt="William Wood, Baron Hatherley &#x2013; Lord Chancellor" title="William Wood, Baron Hatherley &#x2013; Lord Chancellor" /><area href="/wiki/George_Robinson,_1st_Marquess_of_Ripon" shape="circle" coords="187,87,9" alt="George Robinson, Earl de Grey and Ripon &#x2013; Lord President of the Council" title="George Robinson, Earl de Grey and Ripon &#x2013; Lord President of the Council" /><area href="/wiki/Granville_Leveson-Gower,_2nd_Earl_Granville" shape="circle" coords="241,97,12" alt="Granville Leveson-Gower, Earl Granville &#x2013; Colonies" title="Granville Leveson-Gower, Earl Granville &#x2013; Colonies" /><area href="/wiki/John_Wodehouse,_1st_Earl_of_Kimberley" shape="circle" coords="261,95,10" alt="John Wodehouse, Earl of Kimberley &#x2013; Privy Seal" title="John Wodehouse, Earl of Kimberley &#x2013; Privy Seal" /><area href="/wiki/George_Goschen,_1st_Viscount_Goschen" shape="circle" coords="276,102,13" alt="George Goschen &#x2013; Poor Law" title="George Goschen &#x2013; Poor Law" /><area href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" shape="poly" coords="181,141,197,120,197,117,198,114,198,113,198,110,198,109,196,108,196,107,198,104,198,103,197,102,199,98,203,95,212,95,218,99,219,107,216,113,215,114,216,117,222,124,227,133,228,135,219,138,205,138,199,138,195,153,195,165,194,169,173,169,145,169,147,162,151,160,160,159,164,160,162,157,162,152,165,153,163,151,164,150,171,153,175,154,179,149,173,148,171,143,169,143,169,143,171,140,177,140" alt="William Ewart Gladstone &#x2013; Prime Minister" title="William Ewart Gladstone &#x2013; Prime Minister" /><area href="/wiki/Spencer_Cavendish,_8th_Duke_of_Devonshire" shape="circle" coords="53,59,15" alt="Spencer Cavendish, Marquess of Hartington &#x2013; Postmaster General" title="Spencer Cavendish, Marquess of Hartington &#x2013; Postmaster General" /><area href="/wiki/Chichester_Parkinson-Fortescue,_1st_Baron_Carlingford" shape="circle" coords="109,66,12" alt="Chichester Parkinson-Fortescue, Baron Carlingford &#x2013; Ireland" title="Chichester Parkinson-Fortescue, Baron Carlingford &#x2013; Ireland" /><area href="/wiki/Edward_Cardwell,_1st_Viscount_Cardwell" shape="circle" coords="215,67,12" alt="Edward Cardwell &#x2013; Secretary for War" title="Edward Cardwell &#x2013; Secretary for War" /><area href="/wiki/Hugh_Childers" shape="circle" coords="237,63,14" alt="Hugh Childers &#x2013; First Lord of the Admiralty" title="Hugh Childers &#x2013; First Lord of the Admiralty" /><area href="#Victuallers" shape="rect" coords="0,0,299,191" alt="Use your cursor to explore (or click icon to enlarge)" title="Use your cursor to explore (or click icon to enlarge)" /></map><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg/300px-Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg/450px-Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg/600px-Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1535" usemap="#ImageMap_c44d445fd273a19c" /></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gladstone%27s_Cabinet_of_1868_by_Lowes_Cato_Dickinson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gladstone's Cabinet of 1868, painted by Lowes Cato Dickinson.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup> Use a cursor to see who is who.<sup id="cite_ref-shannon_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shannon-68">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Under this form of government, called the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_system" title="Westminster system">Westminster system</a>, the Sovereign is <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and titular head of <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">Her Majesty's Government</a>. She selects as her Prime Minister the person who is able to command a working majority in the House of Commons, and invites him or her to form a government. As the actual <a href="/wiki/Head_of_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Head of Government">Head of Government</a>, the Prime Minister selects his Cabinet, choosing its members from among those in Parliament who agree or generally agree with his intended policies. He then recommends them to the Sovereign who confirms his selections by formally appointing them to their offices. Led by the Prime Minister, the Cabinet is collectively responsible for whatever the government does. The Sovereign does not confer with members privately about policy, nor attend Cabinet meetings. With respect to <i>actual</i> governance, the monarch has only three constitutional rights: to be kept informed, to advise, and to warn.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In practice this means that the Sovereign reviews state papers and meets regularly with the Prime Minister, usually weekly, when she may advise and warn him or her regarding the proposed decisions and actions of Her Government.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loyal_Opposition">Loyal Opposition</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Loyal_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Most Loyal Opposition (United Kingdom)">Her Majesty's Most Loyal Opposition (United Kingdom)</a></div>
<p>The modern British system includes not only a government formed by the majority party (or coalition of parties) in the House of Commons but also an organised and open opposition formed by those who are not members of the governing party.<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett,_p._245_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett,_p._245-50">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Called <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Loyal_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Most Loyal Opposition (United Kingdom)">Her Majesty's Most Loyal Opposition</a>, they occupy the benches to the Speaker's left. Seated in the front, directly across from the ministers on the Treasury Bench, the leaders of the opposition form a "Shadow Government", complete with a salaried "Shadow Prime Minister", the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, ready to assume office if the government falls or loses the next election.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg/220px-House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg/330px-House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg/440px-House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="622" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_of_Commons_Microcosm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The House of Commons early 19th century.</i> The Loyal Opposition occupy the benches to the Speaker's left. Seated in the front, the leaders of the opposition form a "Shadow Government", complete with a salaried "Shadow Prime Minister" ready to assume office if the government falls or loses the next election.</div></div></div>
<p>Opposing the King's government was considered disloyal, even treasonous, at the end of the 17th century. During the 18th century this idea waned and finally disappeared as the two party system developed. The expression "His Majesty's Opposition" was coined by <a href="/wiki/John_Hobhouse,_1st_Baron_Broughton" title="John Hobhouse, 1st Baron Broughton">John Hobhouse, 1st Baron Broughton</a>. In 1826, Broughton, a Whig, announced in the Commons that he opposed the report of a Bill. As a joke, he said, "It was said to be very hard on His Majesty's ministers to raise objections to this proposition. For my part, I think it is much more hard on His Majesty's Opposition to compel them to take this course."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The phrase caught on and has been used ever since. Sometimes rendered as the "<a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Most_Loyal_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Most Loyal Opposition (United Kingdom)">Loyal Opposition</a>", it acknowledges the legitimate existence of the two party system, and describes an important constitutional concept: opposing the government is not treason; reasonable men can honestly oppose its policies and still be loyal to the Sovereign and the nation.
</p><p>Informally recognized for over a century as a convention of the constitution, the position of Leader of the Opposition was given statutory recognition in 1937 by the <a href="/wiki/Ministers_of_the_Crown_Act_1937" title="Ministers of the Crown Act 1937">Ministers of the Crown Act</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Reform_Act_and_the_premiership">Great Reform Act and the premiership</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a></div>
<p>British prime ministers have never been elected directly by the public. A prime minister need not be a party leader; <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> was not a party leader during his service as prime Minister during World War I, and neither was Ramsay MacDonald from 1931 to 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-blake1993_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blake1993-72">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Prime Ministers have taken office because they were members of either the Commons or Lords, and either inherited a majority in the Commons or won more seats than the opposition in a general election.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg/170px-Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg/255px-Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg/340px-Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg 2x" data-file-width="731" data-file-height="1009" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Grey_-_2nd_Earl_Grey_-_atop_the_Grey_Momument_-_Newcastle_upon_Tyne_-_England_-_140804.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Lord Grey</a>, often called the first modern Prime Minister</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg/220px-Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg/330px-Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg/440px-Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Inscription_Grey_Monument.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inscription on <a href="/wiki/Grey%27s_Monument" title="Grey&#39;s Monument">Grey's Monument</a>, Newcastle upon Tyne, England</div></div></div>
<p>Since 1722, most prime ministers have been members of the Commons; since 1902, all have had a seat there.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup> Like other members, they are elected initially to represent only a constituency. Former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, for example, represented <a href="/wiki/Sedgefield_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sedgefield (UK Parliament constituency)">Sedgefield</a> in County Durham from 1983 to 2007. He became Prime Minister because in 1994 he was elected Labour Party leader and then led the party to victory in the <a href="/wiki/1997_United_Kingdom_general_election" title="1997 United Kingdom general election">1997 general election</a>, winning 418 seats compared to 165 for the Conservatives and gaining a majority in the House of Commons.
</p><p>Neither the Sovereign nor the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> had any meaningful influence over who was elected to the Commons in 1997 or in deciding whether or not Blair would become Prime Minister. Their detachment from the electoral process and the selection of the Prime Minister has been a convention of the constitution for almost 200 years.
</p><p>Prior to the 19th century, however, they had significant influence, using to their advantage the fact that most citizens were disenfranchised and seats in the Commons were allocated disproportionately. Through patronage, corruption and bribery, the Crown and Lords "owned" about 30% of the seats (called "pocket" or "rotten boroughs") giving them a significant influence in the Commons and in the selection of the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1830, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Grey,_2nd_Earl_Grey" title="Charles Grey, 2nd Earl Grey">Charles Grey</a>, the 2nd <a href="/wiki/Earl_Grey" title="Earl Grey">Earl Grey</a> and a life-long Whig, became Prime Minister and was determined to reform the electoral system. For two years, he and his Cabinet fought to pass what has come to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Great Reform Bill of 1832</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The greatness of the Great Reform Bill lay less in substance than in symbolism. As John Bright, a liberal statesman of the next generation, said, "It was not a good Bill, but it was a great Bill when it passed."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Substantively, it increased the franchise by 65% to 717,000; with the <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle class</a> receiving most of the new votes. The representation of 56 rotten boroughs was eliminated completely, together with half the representation of 30 others; the freed up seats were distributed to boroughs created for previously disenfranchised areas. However, many rotten boroughs remained and it still excluded millions of working-class men and all women.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Symbolically, however, the Reform Act exceeded expectations. It is now ranked with <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_Rights_1689" title="Bill of Rights 1689">Bill of Rights</a> as one of the most important documents of the British constitutional tradition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>First, the Act removed the sovereign from the election process and the choice of prime minister. Slowly evolving for 100 years, this convention was confirmed two years after the passage of the Act. In 1834, King <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> dismissed Melbourne as premier, but was forced to recall him when <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peel" title="Robert Peel">Robert Peel</a>, the king's choice, could not form a working majority. Since then, no sovereign has tried to impose a prime minister on Parliament.
</p><p>Second, the Bill reduced the Lords' power by eliminating many of their pocket boroughs and creating new boroughs in which they had no influence.
