Weakened, they were unable to prevent the passage of more comprehensive electoral reforms in 1867, 1884, 1918 and 1928 when universal equal suffrage was established.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PunchDizzyReformBill.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PunchDizzyReformBill.png/170px-PunchDizzyReformBill.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PunchDizzyReformBill.png/255px-PunchDizzyReformBill.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/PunchDizzyReformBill.png/340px-PunchDizzyReformBill.png 2x" data-file-width="1218" data-file-height="1589" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PunchDizzyReformBill.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Disraeli and Gladstone Race to Pass the Reform Bill, Punch, 1867</i> The rivalry between Disraeli and Gladstone helped to identify the position of Prime Minister with specific personalities. (Disraeli is in the lead looking back over his shoulder at Gladstone.)</div></div></div>
<p>Ultimately, this erosion of power led to the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>, which marginalised the Lords' role in the legislative process and gave further weight to the convention that had developed over the previous century<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup> that a Prime Minister cannot sit in the House of Lords. The last to do so was <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury</a>, from 1895 to 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup> Throughout the 19th century, governments led from the Lords had often suffered difficulties governing alongside ministers who sat in the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Grey set an example and a precedent for his successors. He was <i>primus inter pares</i> (first among equals), as Bagehot said in 1867 of the Prime Minister's status. Using his Whig victory as a mandate for reform, Grey was unrelenting in the pursuit of this goal, using every parliamentary device to achieve it. Although respectful toward the king, he made it clear that his constitutional duty was to acquiesce to the will of the people and Parliament.
</p><p>The Loyal Opposition acquiesced too. Some disgruntled Tories claimed they would repeal the bill once they regained a majority. But in 1834, Robert Peel, the new Conservative leader, put an end to this threat when he stated in his <a href="/wiki/Tamworth_Manifesto" title="Tamworth Manifesto">Tamworth Manifesto</a> that the bill was "a final and irrevocable settlement of a great constitutional question which no friend to the peace and welfare of this country would attempt to disturb".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Populist_prime_ministers">Populist prime ministers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg/170px-Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg/255px-Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg/340px-Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1314" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Elliott_%26_Fry10a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> cultivated the public image as a <i>man of the people</i> by circulating pictures like this of himself cutting down oak trees with an axe. Photo by <a href="/wiki/Elliott_%26_Fry" title="Elliott &amp; Fry">Elliott &amp; Fry</a></div></div></div>
<p>The premiership was a reclusive office prior to 1832. The incumbent worked with his Cabinet and other government officials; he occasionally met with the sovereign and attended Parliament when it was in session during the spring and summer. He never went out on the stump to campaign, even during elections; he rarely spoke directly to ordinary voters about policies and issues.
</p><p>After the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Reform_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Reform Bill">Great Reform Bill</a>, the nature of the position changed: Prime ministers had to go out among the people. The Bill increased the electorate to 717,000. Subsequent legislation (and population growth) raised it to 2&#160;million in 1867, 5.5&#160;million in 1884 and 21.4&#160;million in 1918. As the franchise increased, power shifted to the people and prime ministers assumed more responsibilities with respect to party leadership. It naturally fell on them to motivate and organise their followers, explain party policies, and deliver its "message". Successful leaders had to have a new set of skills: to give a good speech, present a favourable image, and interact with a crowd. They became the "voice", the "face" and the "image" of the party and ministry.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg/170px-Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg/255px-Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="301" data-file-height="393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Victoria_Disraeli_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> cultivated a public image as an Imperialist with grand gestures such as conferring on <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Victoria</a> the title "Empress of India".</div></div></div>
<p>Robert Peel, often called the "model Prime Minister",<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> was the first to recognise this new role. After the successful Conservative campaign of 1841, J. W. Croker said in a letter to Peel, "The elections are wonderful, and the curiosity is that all turns on the name of Sir Robert Peel. It's the first time that I remember in our history that the people have chosen the first Minister for the Sovereign. Mr. Pitt's case in '84 is the nearest analogy; but then the people only confirmed the Sovereign's choice; here every Conservative candidate professed himself in plain words to be Sir Robert Peel's man, and on that ground was elected."<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Ewart Gladstone</a> developed this new role further by projecting "images" of themselves to the public. Known by their nicknames "Dizzy" and the "Grand Old Man", their colourful, sometimes bitter, personal and political rivalry over the issues of their time – Imperialism vs. Anti-Imperialism, expansion of the franchise, labour reform, and Irish Home Rule – spanned almost twenty years until Disraeli's death in 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;note 9&#93;</a></sup> Documented by the penny press, photographs and political cartoons, their rivalry linked specific personalities with the Premiership in the public mind and further enhanced its status.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GladstoneandRosebery.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/GladstoneandRosebery.gif/220px-GladstoneandRosebery.gif" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/GladstoneandRosebery.gif/330px-GladstoneandRosebery.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/GladstoneandRosebery.gif/440px-GladstoneandRosebery.gif 2x" data-file-width="631" data-file-height="494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GladstoneandRosebery.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Gladstone During the Midlothian Campaign 1879</i> Speaking directly to the people for the first time, Gladstone's Midlothian campaign symbolises a major change in the role of the Prime Minister. (Gladstone is seated in the centre; Rosebery, a future Prime Minister, is sitting on the carpet in front.)</div></div></div>
<p>Each created a different public image of himself and his party. Disraeli, who expanded the Empire to protect British interests abroad, cultivated the image of himself (and the Conservative Party) as "Imperialist", making grand gestures such as conferring the title "Empress of India" on Queen Victoria in 1876. Gladstone, who saw little value in the Empire, proposed an anti-Imperialist policy (later called "Little England"), and cultivated the image of himself (and the Liberal Party) as "man of the people" by circulating pictures of himself cutting down great oak trees with an axe as a hobby.
</p><p>Gladstone went beyond image by appealing directly to the people. In his <a href="/wiki/Midlothian_campaign" title="Midlothian campaign">Midlothian campaign</a> – so called because he stood as a candidate for that county – Gladstone spoke in fields, halls and railway stations to hundreds, sometimes thousands, of students, farmers, labourers and middle class workers. Although not the first leader to speak directly to voters – both he and Disraeli had spoken directly to party loyalists before on special occasions – he was the first to canvass an entire constituency, delivering his message to anyone who would listen, encouraging his supporters and trying to convert his opponents. Publicised nationwide, Gladstone's message became that of the party. Noting its significance, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_7th_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 7th Earl of Shaftesbury">Lord Shaftesbury</a> said, "It is a new thing and a very serious thing to see the Prime Minister on the stump."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Campaigning directly to the people became commonplace. Several 20th century prime ministers, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, were famous for their oratorical skills. After the introduction of radio, motion pictures, television, and the internet, many used these technologies to project their public image and address the nation. <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Baldwin" title="Stanley Baldwin">Stanley Baldwin</a>, a master of the radio broadcast in the 1920s and 1930s, reached a national audience in his talks filled with homely advice and simple expressions of national pride.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Churchill</a> also used the radio to great effect, inspiring, reassuring and informing the people with his speeches during the Second World War. Two recent prime ministers, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> (who both spent a decade or more as prime Minister), achieved celebrity status like rock stars, but have been criticised for their more 'presidential' style of leadership. According to <a href="/wiki/Anthony_King_(professor)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthony King (professor)">Anthony King</a>, "The props in Blair's theatre of celebrity included ... his guitar, his casual clothes ... footballs bounced skilfully off the top of his head ... carefully choreographed speeches and performances at Labour Party conferences."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_premiership">Modern premiership</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parliament_Act_and_the_premiership">Parliament Act and the premiership</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1949" title="Parliament Act 1949">Parliament Act 1949</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Acts_1911_and_1949" title="Parliament Acts 1911 and 1949">Parliament Acts 1911 and 1949</a></div>
<p>In addition to being the leader of a great political party and the head of Her Majesty's Government, the modern Prime Minister directs the law-making process, enacting into law his or her party's programme.  For example, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>, whose Labour party was elected in 1997 partly on a promise to enact a British Bill of Rights and to create devolved governments for Scotland and Wales, subsequently stewarded through Parliament the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Act_(1998)" class="mw-redirect" title="Human Rights Act (1998)">Human Rights Act (1998)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Scotland_Act_(1998)" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotland Act (1998)">Scotland Act (1998)</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Wales_Act_(1998)" class="mw-redirect" title="Government of Wales Act (1998)">Government of Wales Act (1998)</a>.
</p><p>From its appearance in the fourteenth century Parliament has been a bicameral legislature consisting of the Commons and the Lords.  Members of the Commons are elected; those in the Lords are not.  Most Lords are called "Temporal" with titles such as Duke, Marquess, Earl and Viscount.  The balance are Lords Spiritual (prelates of the Anglican Church).
</p><p>For most of the history of the Upper House, Lords Temporal were landowners who held their estates, titles and seats as a hereditary right passed down from one generation to the next – in some cases for centuries.  In 1910, for example, there were nineteen whose title was created before 1500.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;note 10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lords_composition_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lords_composition-94">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until 1911, Prime Ministers had to guide legislation through the Commons and the Lords and obtain majority approval in both houses for it to become law. This was not always easy, because political differences often separated the chambers. Representing the landed aristocracy, Lords Temporal were generally Tory (later Conservative) who wanted to maintain the status quo and resisted progressive measures such as extending the franchise. The party affiliation of members of the Commons was less predictable. During the 18th century its makeup varied because the Lords had considerable control over elections: sometimes Whigs dominated it, sometimes Tories. After the passage of the Great Reform Bill in 1832, the Commons gradually became more progressive, a tendency that increased with the passage of each subsequent expansion of the franchise.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/220px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="196" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/330px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg/440px-Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1348" data-file-height="1202" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_of_Asquith_cabinet19090001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Asquith's Cabinet Reacts to the Lords' Rejection of the "People's Budget"—a satirical cartoon, 1909.</i> Prime Minister Asquith's government welcomed the Lords' veto of the "People's Budget"; it moved the country toward a constitutional crisis over the Lords' legislative powers. (Asquith makes the announcement while David Lloyd George holds down a jubilant Winston Churchill.)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1906, the Liberal party, led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a>, won an overwhelming victory on a platform that promised social reforms for the working class. With 379 seats compared to the Conservatives' 132, the Liberals could confidently expect to pass their legislative programme through the Commons.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> At the same time, however, the Conservative Party had a huge majority in the Lords; it could easily veto any legislation passed by the Commons that was against their interests.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For five years, the Commons and the Lords fought over one bill after another. The Liberals pushed through parts of their programme, but the Conservatives vetoed or modified others. When the Lords vetoed the "<a href="/wiki/People%27s_Budget" title="People&#39;s Budget">People's Budget</a>" in 1909, the controversy moved almost inevitably toward a constitutional crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/220px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/330px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg/440px-Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill%2C_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1554" data-file-height="932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Passing_of_the_Parliament_Bill,_1911_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_19609.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>An important vote: the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> voting for the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a>. From the Drawing by S. Begg</i> The Parliament Act 1911 eliminated the Lords' veto power over legislation approved by the House of Commons. Indirectly, it also further enhanced the dominance of the Prime Minister in the constitutional hierarchy.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1910, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;note 11&#93;</a></sup> introduced a bill "for regulating the relations between the Houses of Parliament" which would eliminate the Lords' veto power over legislation. Passed by the Commons, the Lords rejected it. In a general election fought on this issue, the Liberals were weakened but still had a comfortable majority. At Asquith's request, King <a href="/wiki/George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="George V of the United Kingdom">George V</a> then threatened to create a sufficient number of new Liberal Peers to ensure the bill's passage. Rather than accept a permanent Liberal majority, the Conservative Lords yielded, and the bill became law.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1911" title="Parliament Act 1911">Parliament Act 1911</a> established the supremacy of the Commons. It provided that the Lords could not delay for more than one month any bill certified by the Speaker of the Commons as a money bill. Furthermore, the Act provided that any bill rejected by the Lords would nevertheless become law if passed by the Commons in three successive sessions provided that two years had elapsed since its original passage. The Lords could still delay or suspend the enactment of legislation but could no longer veto it.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Subsequently the Lords "suspending" power was reduced to one year by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1949" title="Parliament Act 1949">Parliament Act 1949</a>.
</p><p>Indirectly, the Act enhanced the already dominant position of Prime Minister in the constitutional hierarchy. Although the Lords are still involved in the legislative process and the Prime Minister must still guide legislation through both Houses, the Lords no longer have the power to veto or even delay enactment of legislation passed by the Commons. Provided that he (or she) controls the Cabinet, maintains party discipline, and commands a majority in the Commons, the Prime Minister is assured of putting through his (or her) legislative agenda.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidentialisation_thesis">Presidentialisation thesis</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"British Presidency" redirects here. For the political units in British India, see <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">Presidencies and provinces of British India</a>.</div><p>The presidentialisation thesis rests on the Prime Minister becoming more detached from Cabinet, party and Parliament and operating as if the occupant of the office is elected directly by the people.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The thesis is usually presented with comparisons to the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">American Presidency</a>. Thomas Poguntke and Paul Webb define it as:
"the development of increasing leadership power resources and autonomy within the party and the political executive respectively, and increasingly leadership-centered electoral processes".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg/220px-Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg/330px-Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg/440px-Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="655" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Churchill_waves_to_crowds.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Churchill waving to crowds after announcing the surrender of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in May 1945.</div></div></div><p>The classic view of Cabinet Government was laid out by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Bagehot" title="Walter Bagehot">Walter Bagehot</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/The_English_Constitution" title="The English Constitution">The English Constitution</a></i> (1867) in which he described the Prime Minister as the <a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus‐inter‐pares</a> ("first among equals").<sup id="cite_ref-Fairclough_2002,_p._~23_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairclough_2002,_p._~23-106">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This view was challenged in <i>The British Cabinet</i> by John P. Mackintosh, who instead used the terminology of <i>Prime Ministerial Government</i> to describe the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> This transformation, according to Mackintosh primarily resulted because of the diminishing role of the Cabinet Ministers and because of centralisation of the party machine and the bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Crossman" title="Richard Crossman">Richard Crossman</a> also alluded to the presidentialisation of British politics in the <i>Introduction</i> to the 1963 version of Walter Bagehot’s <i>The English Constitution</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-108">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Crossman mentions the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and its immediate aftermath as a water-shed moment for Britain that led to immense accumulation of power in the hands of the British Prime Minister<sup id="cite_ref-:1_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-108">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> These powers, according to Crossman, are so immense that their study require the use of presidential parallels.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-108">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The thesis has been most popularised by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foley_(academic)" title="Michael Foley (academic)">Michael Foley</a>, who wrote two books, namely, <i>The Rise of the British Presidency</i>, and <i>The British Presidency: Tony Blair and the Politics of Public Leadership</i> that are solely dedicated to the subject of presidentialisation in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-109">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Foley writes:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The British Prime Minister has to all intents and purposes turned, not into a British version of an American president, but into an authentically British president.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-109">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The thesis has been widely applied to the <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Tony_Blair" title="Premiership of Tony Blair">premiership of Tony Blair</a> as many sources such as former ministers have suggested that decision-making was controlled by him and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>, and the Cabinet was no longer used for decision-making.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Former ministers such as <a href="/wiki/Clare_Short" title="Clare Short">Clare Short</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Smith,_Baron_Smith_of_Finsbury" title="Chris Smith, Baron Smith of Finsbury">Chris Smith</a> have criticised the lack of decision-making power in Cabinet. When she resigned, Short denounced "the centralisation of power into the hands of the Prime Minister and an increasingly small number of advisers".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Graham_Allen_(politician)" title="Graham Allen (politician)">Graham Allen</a> (a Government Whip during Tony Blair's first government) made the case in <i>The Last Prime Minister: Being Honest About the UK Presidency</i> (2003) that in fact the office of prime minister has presidential powers.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the presidentialisation thesis has been extensively criticised as well. <a href="/wiki/Keith_Dowding" title="Keith Dowding">Keith Dowding</a>, for example, argues that British Prime Ministers are already more powerful than the American presidents, as the Prime Minister is part of the legislature, and unlike presidents, can directly initiate legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Thus, Dowding argues that adding to these powers, makes the Prime Minister less like presidents, and that what Britain is witnessing is not presidentialisation but, increase in powers overtime, can be best explained as <i>Prime Ministerialisation</i> of British politics.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>  Regarding the thesis, Dowding writes:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The presidentialisation of the Prime Minister thesis should be expunged from political science vocabulary, to the extent that the forces identified by those who pursue the thesis exist, they do not make the British Prime Minister more like the US president.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-114">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Other academics who have criticised the thesis have pointed to the structural and constitutional differences between Britain and the United States. These authors cite the stark differences between the British parliamentary model, with its principle of <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_sovereignty" title="Parliamentary sovereignty">parliamentary sovereignty</a>, and the American presidential model, which has its roots in the principle of <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a>. For example, according to John Hart, using the American example to explain the accumulation of power in the hands of the British PM is flawed and that changing dynamics of the British executive can only be studied in Britain’s own historical and structural sense.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Moreover, it should also be noted that the power that a Prime Minister has over his or her Cabinet colleagues is directly proportional to the amount of support that they have with their political parties and this is often related to whether the party considers them to be an electoral asset or liability. Additionally, when a party is divided into factions a Prime Minister may be forced to include other powerful party members in the Cabinet for party political cohesion. The Prime Minister's personal power is also curtailed if their party is in a power-sharing arrangement, or a formal coalition with another party (as happened in the coalition government of 2010 to 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-Williams_1998,_pp._113–114_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_1998,_pp._113–114-116">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Powers_and_constraints">Powers and constraints</span></h3>
<p>When commissioned by the Sovereign, a potential Prime Minister's first requisite is to "form a Government" – to create a cabinet of ministers that has the support of the House of Commons, of which they are expected to be a member. The Prime Minister then formally <a href="/wiki/Kissing_hands" title="Kissing hands">kisses the hands</a> of the Sovereign, whose <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Royal prerogative in the United Kingdom">royal prerogative powers</a> are thereafter exercised solely on the advice of the Prime Minister and Her Majesty's Government ("HMG"). The Prime Minister has weekly audiences with the Sovereign, whose rights are constitutionally limited: "to warn, to encourage, and to be consulted";<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> the extent of the Sovereign's ability to influence the nature of the Prime Ministerial advice is unknown, but presumably varies depending upon the personal relationship between the Sovereign and the Prime Minister of the day.
</p><p>The Prime Minister will appoint all other cabinet members (who then become active Privy Counsellors) and ministers, although consulting senior ministers on their junior ministers, without any Parliamentary or other control or process over these powers. At any time, the PM may obtain the appointment, dismissal or nominal resignation of any other minister; the PM may resign, either purely personally or with the whole government. The Prime Minister generally co-ordinates the policies and activities of the Cabinet and Government departments, acting as the main public "face" of Her Majesty's Government.
</p><p>Although the <a href="/wiki/Commander-in-Chief" class="mw-redirect" title="Commander-in-Chief">Commander-in-Chief</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> is legally the Sovereign, under constitutional practice the Prime Minister can declare war, and through the <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Secretary of State for Defence</a> (a position which the PM may appoint, dismiss or even appoint themselves to), as chair of the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Defence Council of the United Kingdom">Defence Council</a>, exert power over the deployment and disposition of the UK's forces.
</p><p>The Prime Minister makes all the most senior Crown appointments, and most others are made by Ministers over whom the PM has the power of appointment and dismissal. <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Counsellors</a>, Ambassadors and <a href="/wiki/High_Commissioner" class="mw-redirect" title="High Commissioner">High Commissioners</a>, senior civil servants, senior military officers, members of important committees and commissions, and other officials are selected, and in most cases may be removed, by the Prime Minister. The PM also formally advises the Sovereign on the appointment of Archbishops and Bishops of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Barnett249_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett249-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> but the PM's discretion is limited by the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Nominations_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Nominations Commission">Crown Nominations Commission</a>. The appointment of senior judges, while constitutionally still on the advice of the Prime Minister, is now made on the basis of recommendations from independent bodies.
</p><p>Peerages, knighthoods, and most other honours are bestowed by the Sovereign only on the advice of the Prime Minister. The only important British honours over which the Prime Minister does not have control are the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Thistle" title="Order of the Thistle">Order of the Thistle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit" title="Order of Merit">Order of Merit</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Companions_of_Honour" title="Order of the Companions of Honour">Order of the Companions of Honour</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victorian_Order" title="Royal Victorian Order">Royal Victorian Order</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Venerable_Order_of_Saint_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Venerable Order of Saint John">Venerable Order of Saint John</a>, which are all within the "personal gift" of the Sovereign.
</p><p>The Prime Minister appoints officials known as the "Government Whips", who negotiate for the support of MPs and to discipline dissenters. Party discipline is strong since electors generally vote for individuals on the basis of their party affiliation. Members of Parliament may be expelled from their party for failing to support the Government on important issues, and although this will not mean they must resign as MPs, it will usually make re-election difficult. Members of Parliament who hold ministerial office or political privileges can expect removal for failing to support the Prime Minister. Restraints imposed by the Commons grow weaker when the Government's party enjoys a large majority in that House, or among the electorate. In most circumstances, however, the Prime Minister can secure the Commons' support for almost any bill by internal party negotiations, with little regard to Opposition MPs.
</p><p>However, even a government with a healthy majority can on occasion find itself unable to pass legislation. For example, on 9 November 2005, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>'s Government was defeated over plans which would have allowed police to detain terror suspects for up to 90 days without charge, and on 31 January 2006, was defeated over certain aspects of proposals to outlaw religious hatred. On other occasions, the Government alters its proposals to avoid defeat in the Commons, as <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>'s Government did in February 2006 over education reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-education_reforms_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-education_reforms-119">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Formerly, a Prime Minister whose government lost a Commons vote would be regarded as fatally weakened, and the whole government would resign, usually precipitating a general election. In modern practice, when the Government party has an absolute majority in the House, only <a href="/wiki/Loss_of_supply" title="Loss of supply">loss of supply</a> and the express vote "that this House has no confidence in Her Majesty's Government" are treated as having this effect; dissenters on a minor issue within the majority party are unlikely to force an election with the probable loss of their seats and salaries.
</p><p>Likewise, a Prime Minister is no longer just "first amongst equals" in HM Government; although theoretically the Cabinet might still outvote the PM, in practice the PM progressively entrenches his or her position by retaining only personal supporters in the Cabinet.  In occasional reshuffles, the Prime Minister can sideline and simply drop from Cabinet the Members who have fallen out of favour: they remain Privy Counsellors, but the Prime Minister decides which of them are summoned to meetings. The Prime Minister is responsible for producing and enforcing the <a href="/wiki/Ministerial_Code" title="Ministerial Code">Ministerial Code</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Precedence.2C_privileges_and_form_of_address"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Precedence,_privileges_and_form_of_address">Precedence, privileges and form of address</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_peerages_held_by_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of peerages held by Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom">List of peerages held by Prime Ministers of the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_precedence_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Orders of precedence in the United Kingdom">Orders of precedence in the United Kingdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chequers2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Chequers2.jpg/220px-Chequers2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Chequers2.jpg/330px-Chequers2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Chequers2.jpg/440px-Chequers2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1771" data-file-height="1108" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chequers2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Chequers_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Chequers Court">Chequers Court</a></i>, the Prime Minister's official country home</div></div></div>
<p>By tradition, before a new Prime Minister can occupy <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a>, they are required to announce to the country and the world that they have "kissed hands" with the reigning monarch, and have thus become Prime Minister. This is usually done by saying words to the effect of:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Her Majesty the Queen <i>[His Majesty the King]</i> has asked me to form a government and I have accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Throughout the United Kingdom, the Prime Minister outranks all other dignitaries except members of the Royal Family, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, and senior ecclesiastical figures.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;note 12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010 the Prime Minister received £142,500 including a salary of £65,737 as a member of parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Until 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> was the highest paid member of the government, ahead of the Prime Minister. This reflected the Lord Chancellor's position at the head of the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_England_and_Wales#Judicial_salaries" title="Judiciary of England and Wales">judicial pay scale</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Reform_Act_2005" title="Constitutional Reform Act 2005">Constitutional Reform Act 2005</a> eliminated the Lord Chancellor's judicial functions and also reduced the office's salary to below that of the Prime Minister.
</p><p>The Prime Minister is customarily a member of the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Privy Council of the United Kingdom">Privy Council</a> and thus entitled to the appellation "<a href="/wiki/The_Right_Honourable" title="The Right Honourable">The Right Honourable</a>". Membership of the Council is retained for life. It is a constitutional convention that only a Privy Counsellor can be appointed Prime Minister. Most potential candidates have already attained this status. The only case when a non-Privy Counsellor was the natural appointment was Ramsay MacDonald in 1924. The issue was resolved by appointing him to the Council immediately prior to his appointment as Prime Minister.
</p><p>According to the now defunct <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Constitutional_Affairs" title="Department for Constitutional Affairs">Department for Constitutional Affairs</a>, the Prime Minister is made a Privy Counsellor as a result of taking office and should be addressed by the official title prefixed by "The Right Honourable" and not by a personal name.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Reliable source needed for the whole sentence (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Although this form of address is employed on formal occasions, it is rarely used by the media. As "Prime Minister" is a position, not a title, the incumbent should be referred to as "the Prime Minister". The title "Prime Minister" (e.g. "Prime Minister Boris Johnson") is technically incorrect but is sometimes used erroneously outside the United Kingdom, and has more recently become acceptable within it.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Within the UK, the expression "Prime Minister Johnson" is never used, although it, too, is sometimes used by foreign dignitaries and news sources.
</p><p>10 Downing Street, in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, has been the <a href="/wiki/Official_residence" title="Official residence">official place of residence</a> of the Prime Minister since 1732; they are entitled to use its staff and facilities, including extensive offices. <a href="/wiki/Chequers" title="Chequers">Chequers</a>, a <a href="/wiki/English_country_house" title="English country house">country house</a> in Buckinghamshire, gifted to the government in 1917, may be used as a country retreat for the Prime Minister.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Living_former_prime_ministers">Living former prime ministers</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Living_prime_ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Living prime ministers of the United Kingdom">Living prime ministers of the United Kingdom</a></div>

</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_Honours">Retirement Honours</span></h4>
<p>Upon retirement, it is customary for the Sovereign to grant a Prime Minister some honour or dignity. The honour bestowed is commonly, but not invariably, membership of the UK's most senior order of chivalry, the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>. The practice of creating a retired (male) Prime Minister a Knight of the Garter (KG) has been fairly prevalent since the mid–nineteenth century. Upon the retirement of a Prime Minister who is Scottish, it is likely that the primarily Scottish honour of <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Thistle" title="Order of the Thistle">Knight of the Thistle</a> (KT) will be used instead of the Order of the Garter, which is generally regarded as an English honour.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;note 13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically it has also been common to grant prime ministers a <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peerage</a> upon retirement from the Commons, elevating the individual to the Lords. Formerly, the peerage bestowed was usually an <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earldom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;note 14&#93;</a></sup> The last such creation was for <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>, who resigned in 1963. Unusually, he became <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Stockton" title="Earl of Stockton">Earl of Stockton</a> only in 1984, over twenty years after leaving office.
</p><p>Macmillan's successors, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> all accepted <a href="/wiki/Life_peer" title="Life peer">life peerages</a> (although Douglas-Home had previously disclaimed his hereditary title as <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Home" title="Earl of Home">Earl of Home</a>). <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> did not accept a peerage of any kind and nor have any of the prime ministers to retire since 1990; although Heath and Major were later appointed as Knights of the Garter.
</p><p>The most recent former Prime Minister to die was Margaret Thatcher (1979–1990) on 8 April 2013. Her death meant that for the first time since 1955 (the year in which the <a href="/wiki/Earl_Attlee" title="Earl Attlee">Earldom of Attlee</a> was created, subsequent to the death of <a href="/wiki/Earl_Baldwin_of_Bewdley" title="Earl Baldwin of Bewdley">Earl Baldwin</a> in 1947) the membership of the House of Lords included no former Prime Minister, a situation which remains the case.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Sovereign's prerogative powers are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/Reserve_power" title="Reserve power">reserve powers</a>. They include the sole authority to dismiss a Prime Minister and government of the day in extremely rare and exceptional circumstances, and other essential powers (such as withholding <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a>, and summoning and proroguing Parliament) to preserve the stability of the nation. These reserve powers can be exercised without the consent of Parliament. Reserve powers, in practice, are the court of absolute last resort in resolving situations that fundamentally threaten the security and stability of the nation as a whole and are almost never used.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Once in office, the Prime Minister fills not only Cabinet level positions but many other government offices (up to 90 appointments), selected mostly from the House of Commons, distributing them to party members, partly as a reward for their loyalty.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The power to make so many appointments to government offices is one of the most effective means the Prime Minister has of maintaining party discipline in the Commons.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See e.g. the various orders prescribing fees to be taken in public offices</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The 18th-century ambivalence causes problems for researchers trying to identify who was a Prime Minister and who was not. Every list of Prime Ministers may omit certain politicians. For instance, unsuccessful attempts to form ministries – such as the two-day government formed by the <a href="/wiki/William_Pulteney,_1st_Earl_of_Bath" title="William Pulteney, 1st Earl of Bath">Earl of Bath</a> in 1746, often dismissed as the "<a href="/wiki/Short-lived_Ministry" class="mw-redirect" title="Short-lived Ministry">Silly Little Ministry</a>" – may be included in a list or omitted, depending on the criteria selected.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This event also marks the beginnings of collective Cabinet responsibility. This principle states that the decisions made by any one Cabinet member become the responsibility of the entire Cabinet.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Except <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Lord Home</a>, who resigned his peerage to stand in a by-election soon after becoming Prime Minister</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As early as 1839, the former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington</a> had argued in the House of Lords that "I have long entertained the view that the Prime Minister of this country, under existing circumstances, ought to have a seat in the other House of Parliament, and that he would have great advantage in carrying on the business of the Sovereign by being there." Quoted in <a href="#Barnett">Barnett</a>, p. 246</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The last Prime Minister to be a member of the Lords during any part of his tenure was <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home,_14th_Earl_of_Home" class="mw-redirect" title="Alec Douglas-Home, 14th Earl of Home">Alec Douglas-Home, 14th Earl of Home</a> in 1963. Lord Home was the last Prime Minister who was a hereditary peer, but, within days of attaining office, he disclaimed his peerage, abiding by the convention that the Prime Minister should sit in the House of Commons. A junior member of his Conservative Party who had already been selected as candidate in a <a href="/wiki/1963_Kinross_and_Western_Perthshire_by-election" title="1963 Kinross and Western Perthshire by-election">by-election</a> in a staunch Conservative seat stood aside, allowing Home to contest and win the by-election, and thus procure a seat in the lower House.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Even after death their rivalry continued. When Disraeli died in 1881, Gladstone proposed a state funeral, but Disraeli's will specified that he have a private funeral and be buried next to his wife. Gladstone replied, "As [Disraeli] lived, so he died—all display, without reality or genuineness." Disraeli, for his part, once said that GOM (the acronym for "Grand Old Man") really stood for "God's Only Mistake".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-93"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-93">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Following a series of reforms in the twentieth century the Lords now consists almost entirely of appointed members who hold their title only for their own lifetime.  As of 11 June 2012 the Lords had 763 members (excluding 49 who were on leave of absence or otherwise disqualified from sitting), compared to 646 in the Commons.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Campbell-Bannerman retired and died in 1908</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These include: in <a href="/wiki/England_and_Wales" title="England and Wales">England and Wales</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Anglican</a> Archbishops of Canterbury and York; in Scotland, the Lord High Commissioner and the <a href="/wiki/Moderator_of_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="Moderator of the General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">Moderator of the General Assembly of the Church of Scotland</a>; in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Anglican</a> and Roman Catholic Archbishops of Armagh and Dublin and the <a href="/wiki/Moderator_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Moderator of the Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Moderator of the General Assembly</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This circumstance is somewhat confused, however, as since the Great Reform Act 1832, only seven Scots have served as Prime Minister. Of these, two – <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> – died while still sitting in the Commons, not yet having retired; another, the <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Earl of Aberdeen</a>, was appointed to <i>both</i> the Order of the Garter and the Order of the Thistle; yet another, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, was appointed to the Order of the Garter, but represented an English constituency and may not have considered himself entirely Scottish; and of the remaining three, the <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Earl of Rosebery</a> became a KG, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a> became a KT, and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> remained in the House of Commons as a <a href="/wiki/Backbencher" title="Backbencher">backbencher</a> until 2015.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Churchill was offered a <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of London">dukedom</a> but declined.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As <a href="/wiki/First_Lord_of_the_Treasury" title="First Lord of the Treasury">First Lord of the Treasury</a> and <i>de facto</i> first Prime Minister.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gov.uk/government/ministers/prime-minister">"Prime Minister"</a>. <i>Gov.UK</i>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20171014185426/https://www.gov.uk/government/ministers/prime-minister">Archived</a> from the original on 14 October 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">19 May</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Gov.UK&amp;rft.atitle=Prime+Minister&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.gov.uk%2Fgovernment%2Fministers%2Fprime-minister&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrime+Minister+of+the+United+Kingdom" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Le_May">Le May</a>, 98–99. Walter Bagehot, an authority on 19th-century British government, said this unity is "the efficient secret" of its constitution. Bagehot's description of the "efficient part" of the British constitution is quoted by Le May and many other standard texts: "The efficient secret of the English Constitution may be described as the close union, the nearly complete fusion, of the executive and legislative powers. No doubt, by the traditional theory, as it exists in all the books, the goodness of our constitution consists in the entire separation of the legislative and executive authorities, but in truth its merit consists in their singular approximation. The connecting link is the Cabinet ... A Cabinet is a combing committee—a hyphen which joins a buckle which fastens the legislative part of the State to the executive part of the State. In its origin it belongs to the one, in its functions it belongs to the other."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Barnett">Barnett</a>, pp. 245–246</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#King">King</a>, pp. 3–8. King makes the point that much of the British constitution is in fact written and that no constitution is written down in its entirety. The distinctive feature of the British constitution, he says, is that it is not codified.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Quoted in <a href="#Hanchant">Hanchant</a>, p. 209</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Low">Low</a>, p.155. In 1902, for example, Arthur Balfour said, "The Prime Minister has no salary as Prime Minister. He has no statutory duties as Prime Minister, his name occurs in no Acts of Parliament, and though holding the most important place in the constitutional hierarchy, he has no place which is recognized by the laws of his country. This is a strange paradox"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Low">Low</a>, p. 255 "There is no distinction," said Gladstone, "more vital to the practice of the British constitution or to the right judgement upon it than the distinction between the Sovereign and the Crown."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Bagehot">Bagehot</a>, p. 67</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Low">Low</a>, pp 255–258</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Knappen">Knappen</a>, pp. 448–451</span>
</li>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, p. 382</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/cm200910/cmstords/2/2.pdf">"Standing Orders of the House of Commons"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. London, United Kingdom: Parliament of the United Kingdom. 16 December 2009. p.&#160;65.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Standing+Orders+of+the+House+of+Commons&amp;rft.place=London%2C+United+Kingdom&amp;rft.pages=65&amp;rft.pub=Parliament+of+the+United+Kingdom&amp;rft.date=2009-12-16&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fpublications.parliament.uk%2Fpa%2Fcm200910%2Fcmstords%2F2%2F2.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APrime+Minister+of+the+United+Kingdom" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Roseveare">Roseveare</a>, p. 80</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, pp. 372–373</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dodd">Dodd</a>, p. 50 There are a few instances of the use of "Prime" or "First" Minister in the 17th century. After the Restoration in 1660, for example, Lord Clarendon was encouraged to assume the title of "First Minister" in the new government rather than accept a specific office. According to the Duke of Ormonde, however, "He (Clarendon) could not consent to enjoy a pension out of the Exchequer under no other title or pretense but being First Minister... [an office] so newly translated out of French into English that it was not enough understood to be liked and everyone would detest it for the burden it was attended with."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Marriott">Marriott</a>, p. 87</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Barnett">Barnett</a>, p. 247</span>

<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Jennings">Jennings</a>, p. 59</span>

<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Dodd">Dodd</a>, p. 79. In 1691, for example, a Lord protested, that <span style="padding-right:.15em;">"</span>'Cabinet-Council' is not a word to be found in our Law-books. We know it not before: we took it for a nick-name. Nothing can fall out more unhappily, than to have a distinction made of the 'Cabinet' and 'Privy-Council' ... If some of the Privy-Council men be trusted, and some not, to whom is a gentleman to apply? Must he ask, "Who is a Cabinet-Counsellor? ... I am sure, these distinctions of some being more trusted than others have given great dissatisfaction."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, p. 385. He worked tirelessly to maintain the king's confidence, and sometimes resorted to bribery. On the accession of George II in 1727, for example, Walpole gave the new king an additional £100,000 for his personal use to maintain his offices.</span>

<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Marriott">Marriott</a>, pp. 77–81. During most periods of British history, there have been Chief Ministers who have had many of the attributes of a modern prime minister such as <a href="/wiki/Dunstan_of_Glastonbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunstan of Glastonbury">Dunstan of Glastonbury</a> under <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_Peaceful" title="Edgar the Peaceful">Edgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ranulf_Flambard" title="Ranulf Flambard">Ranulf Flambard</a> under <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Wolsey" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardinal Wolsey">Cardinal Wolsey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cromwell" title="Thomas Cromwell">Thomas Cromwell</a> under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, and many others.</span>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Low">Low</a>, pp. 160–161. In his memoirs, <i>Gleanings</i>, Gladstone lamented the prime ministry's unseemly status in the government hierarchy: "Nowhere in the wide world," he said, "does so great a substance cast so small a shadow. Nowhere is there a man who has so much power with so little to show for it in the way of formal title or prerogative."</span>

<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Marriott">Marriott</a>, pp 78–83. Marriott enumerates five characteristics of modern Cabinet Government: 1. exclusion of the Sovereign, 2. close correspondence of party affiliation between the Cabinet and the majority in Parliament, 3. homogeneity of the Cabinet, 4. collective responsibility, and 5. ascendency of the Prime Minister.</span>

"My honourable friend," he said, "could not have invented a better phrase to designate us than that which he has adopted, for we are certainly to all intents and purposes a branch of His Majesty's Government."</span>

<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minney, p. 216. Contemporaries seemed to sense from the beginning that history was being made. Lord Creevey, for example, recorded in his diary, "I dined in Downing Street with Lady Grey... After dinner the private secretary to the Prime Minister and myself being alone, I ascertained that although Lord Grey was gone to Brighton ostensibly to prick for Sheriffs for the year, his great object was to put his plan of reform before the King, previous... to its being proposed to the House of Commons. A ticklish operation, this! to propose to a Sovereign a plan for reducing his own power and patronage. However, there is the plan all cut and dry, and the Cabinet unanimous upon it... Grey is determined to fight it out to a dissolution of Parliament, if his plan is beat in the Commons. My eye, what a crisis!"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Trevelyan, p.272</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Marriott">Marriott</a>, pp. 222–223</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-80">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, pp. 437–444</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, pp. 454, 468, 486, and 489</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Jennings">Jennings</a>, p. 21</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Pike">Pike</a>, p. 219</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Rosebery, p. 27. Lord Rosebery, later a Prime Minister himself, said of Peel: "the model of all Prime Ministers. It is more than doubtful, indeed, if it be possible in this generation, when the burdens of Empire and of office have so incalculably grown, for any Prime Minister to discharge the duties of his high office with the same thoroughness or in the same spirit as Peel. ... Peel kept a strict supervision over every department: he seems to have been master of the business of each and all of them. ... it is probable that no Prime Minister ever fulfilled so completely and thoroughly the functions of the office, parliamentary, administrative, and general as Sir Robert Peel."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hanham, pp. 63–64</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bigham, p. 318. Disraeli and Victoria thought the tactic was unconstitutional. "Such conduct", the Queen said, "is unheard of and the only excuse is—that he is not quite sane."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Pike">Pike</a>, p. 389</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#King">King</a>, pp. 319–320</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Tuchman1">Tuchman</a>, p 391</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-lords_composition-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-lords_composition_94-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080514004118/http://www.parliament.uk/directories/house_of_lords_information_office/analysis_by_composition.cfm">"House of Lords: Breakdown of Lords by party strength and type of peerage"</a>. 1 May 2008. <li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080511151016/http://www.parliament.uk/directories/hcio/stateparties.cfm">"House of Commons: State of the parties"</a>. 23 May 2008. <li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, p. 477,</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Tuchman1">Tuchman</a>, p 365. The Liberal majority was actually much larger in practice because on most issues they could rely on the votes of 51 Labour and Lib-Lab representatives and 83 Irish Nationalists. Their majority was so large and unprecedented – they had more seats than all other parties combined – that one Conservative called it a "hideous abnormality".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a>, the defeated Conservative Prime Minister and now Leader of the Opposition, declared that the House of Lords was the "watchdog of the constitution"; it had an obligation to promote stability by rejecting "radical" legislation proposed by "zealots" who may have a temporary numerical advantage in the Commons. <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, the new Liberal <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a> and a future Prime Minister, said the Lords "... is not the watchdog of the British Constitution. It is Mr Balfour's poodle!" <a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, p. 478</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Smith">Smith</a>, pp 478–480. Although the Liberals did pass the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Disputes_Act_1906" title="Trade Disputes Act 1906">Trade Disputes Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Workmen%27s_Compensation_Act_1906" title="Workmen&#39;s Compensation Act 1906">Workmen's Compensation Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Exchanges_Act_1909" title="Labour Exchanges Act 1909">Labour Exchanges Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Trade_Boards_Act_1909" title="Trade Boards Act 1909">Trade Boards Act</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Housing,_Town_Planning,_%26c._Act_1909" title="Housing, Town Planning, &amp;c. Act 1909">Housing, Town Planning, &amp;c. Act</a>, the Lords vetoed an <a href="/wiki/Education_Bill" class="mw-redirect" title="Education Bill">Education Bill</a>, a land reform bill, a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Licensing_Bill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Licensing Bill (page does not exist)">Licensing Bill</a>, and a Plural Voting Bill; they mutilated and mauled an <a href="/w/index.php?title=Agricultural_Holdings_Bill&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Agricultural Holdings Bill (page does not exist)">Agricultural Holdings Bill</a> and an Irish Town Tenants Bill, and they almost rejected the <a href="/wiki/Old-Age_Pensions_Act_1908" title="Old-Age Pensions Act 1908">Old-Age Pensions Act</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Knappen">Knappen</a>, pp 554–555</span>
